title="Help:IPA/Korean">[tʰɛ.k͈wʌn.do]</a></span>&#32;<span class="nowrap" style="font-size:85%">(<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap;margin-right:.25em;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ko-%ED%83%9C%EA%B6%8C%EB%8F%84.oga" title="About this sound"><img alt="About this sound" 2x" data-file-width="20" data-file-height="20" /></a></span><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c7/Ko-%ED%83%9C%EA%B6%8C%EB%8F%84.oga" class="internal" title="Ko-태권도.oga">listen</a></span></span>)</span></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Taekwondo</b> or <b>Tae Kwon Do</b> (<a href="/wiki/Korean_language" title="Korean language">Korean</a> <span lang="ko" title="Korean language text">태권도/跆拳道</span> <small></small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Korean" title="Help:IPA/Korean">[tʰɛ.k͈wʌn.do]</a></span>&#32;<span class="nowrap" style="font-size:85%">(<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap;margin-right:.25em;"><a href="/wiki/File:Ko-%ED%83%9C%EA%B6%8C%EB%8F%84.oga" title="About this sound"><img alt="About this sound" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" decoding="async" width="11" height="11" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="20" data-file-height="20" /></a></span><a href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/c7/Ko-%ED%83%9C%EA%B6%8C%EB%8F%84.oga" class="internal" title="Ko-태권도.oga">listen</a></span></span>)</span>, <span style="font-size:85%;">English pronunciation</span> <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˌ/: secondary stress follows">ˌ</span><span title="&#39;t&#39; in &#39;tie&#39;">t</span><span title="/aɪ/: &#39;i&#39; in &#39;tide&#39;">aɪ</span><span title="&#39;k&#39; in &#39;kind&#39;">k</span><span title="&#39;w&#39; in &#39;wind&#39;">w</span><span title="/ɒ/: &#39;o&#39; in &#39;body&#39;">ɒ</span><span title="&#39;n&#39; in &#39;nigh&#39;">n</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="&#39;d&#39; in &#39;dye&#39;">d</span><span title="/oʊ/: &#39;o&#39; in &#39;code&#39;">oʊ</span></span>/</a></span></span>,<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup> <span style="font-size:85%;">or</span> <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/ˌ/: secondary stress follows">ˌ</span><span title="&#39;t&#39; in &#39;tie&#39;">t</span><span title="/aɪ/: &#39;i&#39; in &#39;tide&#39;">aɪ</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="&#39;k&#39; in &#39;kind&#39;">k</span><span title="&#39;w&#39; in &#39;wind&#39;">w</span><span title="/ɒ/: &#39;o&#39; in &#39;body&#39;">ɒ</span><span title="&#39;n&#39; in &#39;nigh&#39;">n</span><span title="&#39;d&#39; in &#39;dye&#39;">d</span><span title="/oʊ/: &#39;o&#39; in &#39;code&#39;">oʊ</span></span>/</a></span></span><sup id="cite_ref-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-3">&#91;3&#93;</a></sup>) is a <a href="/wiki/Korean_martial_arts" title="Korean martial arts">Korean martial art</a>, characterized by its emphasis on head-height kicks, jumping and spinning kicks, and fast kicking techniques.
</p><p>Taekwondo is a combative sport and was developed during the 1940s and 1950s by Korean martial artists with experience in martial arts such as <a href="/wiki/Karate" title="Karate">karate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chinese_martial_arts" title="Chinese martial arts">Chinese martial arts</a>, and indigenous Korean martial arts traditions such as <a href="/wiki/Taekkyeon" title="Taekkyeon">Taekkyon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Subak" title="Subak">Subak</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Gwonbeop" title="Gwonbeop">Gwonbeop</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;4&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Brief_History_of_Taekwondo_5-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brief_History_of_Taekwondo-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> The oldest governing body for Taekwondo is the <a href="/wiki/Korea_Taekwondo_Association" title="Korea Taekwondo Association">Korea Taekwondo Association</a> (KTA), formed in 1959 through a collaborative effort by representatives from the nine original <a href="/wiki/Kwan_(martial_arts)#The_five_original_Taekwondo_kwans" title="Kwan (martial arts)">kwans</a>, or martial arts schools, in Korea. The main international organisational bodies for Taekwondo today are the <a href="/wiki/International_Taekwon-Do_Federation" title="International Taekwon-Do Federation">International Taekwon-Do Federation</a> (ITF), founded by <a href="/wiki/Choi_Hong_Hi" title="Choi Hong Hi">Choi Hong Hi</a> in 1966, and the partnership of the <a href="/wiki/Kukkiwon" title="Kukkiwon">Kukkiwon</a> and <a href="/wiki/World_Taekwondo" title="World Taekwondo">World Taekwondo</a> (WT, formerly WTF), founded in 1972 and 1973 respectively by the <a href="/wiki/Korea_Taekwondo_Association" title="Korea Taekwondo Association">Korea Taekwondo Association</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-KANG_6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KANG-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> <i>Gyeorugi</i> (<small></small><span title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)" class="IPA"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Korean" title="Help:IPA/Korean">[kjʌɾuɡi]</a></span>), a type of full-contact <a href="/wiki/Sparring" title="Sparring">sparring</a>, has been an <a href="/wiki/Taekwondo_at_the_Summer_Olympics" title="Taekwondo at the Summer Olympics">Olympic event</a> since 2000.  The governing body for Taekwondo in the <a href="/wiki/Olympic_Games" title="Olympic Games">Olympics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Paralympic_Games" title="Paralympic Games">Paralympics</a> is World Taekwondo.
</p>

</ul>
</li>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taekwondo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Korean_martial_arts" title="Korean martial arts">Korean martial arts</a></div>
<p>Beginning in 1945, shortly after the end of <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a> and <a href="/wiki/Korea_under_Japanese_rule" title="Korea under Japanese rule">Japanese Occupation</a>, new <a href="/wiki/Martial_arts" title="Martial arts">martial arts</a> schools called <i><a href="/wiki/Kwan_(martial_arts)" title="Kwan (martial arts)">kwans</a></i> opened in <a href="/wiki/Seoul" title="Seoul">Seoul</a>. These schools were established by Korean martial artists with backgrounds in (mostly) Japanese<sup id="cite_ref-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-7">&#91;7&#93;</a></sup> and Chinese martial arts. At the time, indigenous disciplines (such as <a href="/wiki/Taekkyeon" title="Taekkyeon">Taekkyon</a>) were all but forgotten, due to years of decline and repression by the colonial government. The umbrella term <i>traditional Taekwondo</i> typically refers to the martial arts practiced by the kwans during the 1940s and 1950s, though in reality the term "Taekwondo" had not yet been coined at that time, and indeed each Kwan (martial art school) was practicing its own unique style of martial art.
</p><p>In 1952, <a href="/wiki/South_Korea" title="South Korea">South Korean</a> <a href="/wiki/President_of_South_Korea" title="President of South Korea">President</a> <a href="/wiki/Syngman_Rhee" title="Syngman Rhee">Syngman Rhee</a> witnessed a pivotal <a href="/wiki/Martial_arts" title="Martial arts">martial arts</a> demonstration by ROK Officer <a href="/wiki/Choi_Hong_Hi" title="Choi Hong Hi">Choi Hong-hi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nam_Tae_Hi" title="Nam Tae Hi">Nam Tae-hi</a> from the 29th Infantry Division. He misrecognized the technique on display as '''<a href="/wiki/Taekkyeon" title="Taekkyeon">Taekkyon</a>, the art transmitted by our ancestors"<sup id="cite_ref-Gillis_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gillis-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-:2_9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:2-9">&#91;9&#93;</a></sup> and urged martial arts to be introduced to the army under a single system. Beginning in 1955 the leaders of the kwans began discussing in earnest the possibility of creating a unified style of Korean martial arts. The name <i><a href="/wiki/Tae_Soo_Do" title="Tae Soo Do">Tae Soo Do</a></i>/<i>Tang Soo Do</i> was used to describe this unified style. This name consists of the <a href="/wiki/Hanja" title="Hanja">hanja</a> <span lang="ko" title="Korean language text"><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E8%B7%86" class="extiw" title="wikt:跆">跆</a></span> <i>tae</i> "to stomp, trample", <span lang="ko" title="Korean language text"><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E6%89%8B" class="extiw" title="wikt:手">手</a></span> <i>su</i> "hand" and <span lang="ko" title="Korean language text"><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E9%81%93" class="extiw" title="wikt:道">道</a></span> <i>do</i> "<a href="/wiki/D%C5%8D_(martial_arts)" title="Dō (martial arts)">way, discipline</a>".
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Choi_Hong_Hi" title="Choi Hong Hi">Choi Hong Hi</a> advocated the use of the name <i>Tae Kwon Do</i>, i.e. replacing <i>su</i> "hand" by <span lang="ko" title="Korean language text"><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E6%8B%B3" class="extiw" title="wikt:拳">拳</a></span> <i>kwon</i> (<a href="/wiki/Revised_Romanization_of_Korean" title="Revised Romanization of Korean">Revised Romanization</a>: <i>gwon</i>; <a href="/wiki/McCune%E2%80%93Reischauer" title="McCune–Reischauer">McCune–Reischauer</a>: <i>kkwŏn</i>) "fist", the term also used for "martial arts" in <a href="/wiki/Chinese_language" title="Chinese language">Chinese</a> (<a href="/wiki/Pinyin" title="Pinyin">pinyin</a> <i>quán</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-:3_10-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:3-10">&#91;10&#93;</a></sup> The name was also the closest to the pronunciation of Taekkyon,<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">&#91;11&#93;</a></sup> in accordance with the views of the president.<sup id="cite_ref-Gillis_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gillis-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">&#91;12&#93;</a></sup> The new name was initially slow to catch on among the leaders of the kwans. During this time Taekwondo was also adopted for use by the <a href="/wiki/Republic_of_Korea_Armed_Forces" title="Republic of Korea Armed Forces">South Korean military</a>, which increased its popularity among civilian martial arts schools.<sup id="cite_ref-KANG_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KANG-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Gillis_8-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gillis-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1959 the <a href="/wiki/Korea_Taekwondo_Association" title="Korea Taekwondo Association">Korea Taekwondo Association</a> (then-Korea Tang Soo Do Association) was established to facilitate the unification of Korean martial arts. General Choi, of the Oh Do Kwan, wanted all the other member kwans of the KTA to adopt his own Chan Hon-style of Taekwondo, as a unified style. This was, however, met with resistance as the other kwans instead wanted a unified style to be created based on inputs from all the kwans, to serve as a way to bring on the heritage and characteristics of all of the styles, not just the style of a single kwan.<sup id="cite_ref-KANG_6-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KANG-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> As a response to this, along with disagreements about teaching Taekwondo in North Korea and unifying the whole Korean Peninsula, Choi broke with the KTA in 1966, in order to establish the <a href="/wiki/International_Taekwon-Do_Federation" title="International Taekwon-Do Federation">International Taekwon-Do Federation</a> (ITF)— a separate governing body devoted to institutionalizing his own style of Taekwondo in Canada.<sup id="cite_ref-KANG_6-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KANG-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Gillis_8-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gillis-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Initially, the South Korean president, having close ties to General Choi, gave General Choi's ITF limited support.<sup id="cite_ref-KANG_6-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KANG-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> However, the South Korean government wished to avoid <a href="/wiki/North_Korea" title="North Korea">North Korean</a> influence on the martial art. Conversely, ITF president <a href="/wiki/Choi_Hong_Hi" title="Choi Hong Hi">Choi Hong Hi</a> sought support for his style of Taekwondo from all quarters, including North Korea. In response, in 1972 South Korea withdrew its support for the ITF. The ITF continued to function as an independent federation, then headquartered in <a href="/wiki/Toronto" title="Toronto">Toronto</a>, Ontario, Canada; Choi continued to develop the ITF-style, notably with the 1987 publication of his <i>Encyclopedia of Taekwondo</i>. After Choi's retirement, the ITF split in 2001 and then again in 2002 to create three separate federations each of which continues to operate today under the same name.<sup id="cite_ref-KANG_6-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KANG-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1972 the KTA and the South Korean government's <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Culture,_Sports_and_Tourism" title="Ministry of Culture, Sports and Tourism">Ministry of Culture, Sports and Tourism</a> established the <a href="/wiki/Kukkiwon" title="Kukkiwon">Kukkiwon</a> as the new national academy for Taekwondo. Kukkiwon now serves many of the functions previously served by the KTA, in terms of defining a government-sponsored unified style of Taekwondo. In 1973 the KTA and Kukkiwon supported the establishment of the <a href="/wiki/World_Taekwondo" title="World Taekwondo">World Taekwondo Federation</a> (WTF, renamed to World Taekwondo in 2017 due to confusion with the initialism<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">&#91;13&#93;</a></sup>) to promote Taekwondo specifically as an international sport. WT competitions employ Kukkiwon-style Taekwondo.<sup id="cite_ref-KANG_6-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KANG-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Kukkiwon_14-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kukkiwon-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> For this reason, Kukkiwon-style Taekwondo is often referred to as WT-style Taekwondo, sport-style Taekwondo, or Olympic-style Taekwondo, though in reality the style is defined by the Kukkiwon, not the <a href="/wiki/World_Taekwondo" title="World Taekwondo">WT</a>.
</p><p>Since 2000, Taekwondo has been one of only two Asian martial arts (the other being <a href="/wiki/Judo" title="Judo">judo</a>) that are included in the <a href="/wiki/Olympic_Games" title="Olympic Games">Olympic Games</a>. It started as a demonstration event at the 1988 games in Seoul, a year after becoming a medal event at the <a href="/wiki/Taekwondo_at_the_Pan_American_Games" title="Taekwondo at the Pan American Games">Pan Am Games</a>, and became an official medal event at the 2000 games in Sydney. In 2010, Taekwondo was accepted as a <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_Games" title="Commonwealth Games">Commonwealth Games</a> sport.
<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">&#91;15&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Features">Features</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taekwondo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Features">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Flying_Double_Side_Kick_in_Martial_Arts.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Flying_Double_Side_Kick_in_Martial_Arts.JPG/250px-Flying_Double_Side_Kick_in_Martial_Arts.JPG" decoding="async" width="250" height="250" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Flying_Double_Side_Kick_in_Martial_Arts.JPG/375px-Flying_Double_Side_Kick_in_Martial_Arts.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Flying_Double_Side_Kick_in_Martial_Arts.JPG/500px-Flying_Double_Side_Kick_in_Martial_Arts.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2222" data-file-height="2222" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Flying_Double_Side_Kick_in_Martial_Arts.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Flying twin foot side kick</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Master_Scott_Leonard.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/42/Master_Scott_Leonard.jpg/250px-Master_Scott_Leonard.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="194" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/42/Master_Scott_Leonard.jpg/375px-Master_Scott_Leonard.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/4/42/Master_Scott_Leonard.jpg/500px-Master_Scott_Leonard.jpg 2x" data-file-width="604" data-file-height="468" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Master_Scott_Leonard.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A jumping reverse hook kick</div></div></div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Taekwondo_techniques" title="List of Taekwondo techniques">List of Taekwondo techniques</a></div>
<p>Taekwondo is characterized by its emphasis on head-height kicks, jumping and spinning kicks, and fast kicking techniques. In fact, <a href="/wiki/World_Taekwondo" title="World Taekwondo">World Taekwondo</a> sparring competitions award additional points for strikes that incorporate spinning kicks, kicks to the head, or both.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">&#91;16&#93;</a></sup> To facilitate fast, turning kicks, Taekwondo generally adopts stances that are narrower and taller than the broader, wide stances used by martial arts such as karate. The tradeoff of decreased stability is believed to be worth the commensurate increase in agility, particularly in Kukkiwon-style Taekwondo.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Theory_of_power">Theory of power</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taekwondo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Theory of power">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The emphasis on speed and agility is a defining characteristic of Taekwondo and has its origins in analyses undertaken by <a href="/wiki/Choi_Hong_Hi" title="Choi Hong Hi">Choi Hong Hi</a>. The results of that analysis are known by ITF practitioners as Choi's <i>Theory of Power</i>. Choi based his understanding of power on <a href="/wiki/Biomechanics" title="Biomechanics">biomechanics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Classical_mechanics" title="Classical mechanics">Newtonian physics</a> as well as Chinese martial arts. For example, Choi observed that the kinetic energy of a strike increases quadratically with the speed of the strike, but increases only linearly with the mass of the striking object. In other words, speed is more important than size in terms of generating power. This principle was incorporated into the early design of Taekwondo and is still used.
<sup id="cite_ref-Gillis_8-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gillis-8">&#91;8&#93;</a></sup>
<sup id="cite_ref-Choi_17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Choi-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Choi also advocated a <i>relax/strike</i> principle for Taekwondo; in other words, between blocks, kicks, and strikes the practitioner should relax the body, then tense the muscles only while performing the technique. It is believed that the relax/strike principle increases the power of the technique, by conserving the body's energy. He expanded on this principle with his advocacy of the <i>sine wave</i> technique. This involves raising one's centre of gravity between techniques, then lowering it as the technique is performed, producing the up-and-down movement from which the term "sine wave" is derived.
<sup id="cite_ref-Choi_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Choi-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>
The <i>sine wave</i> is generally practiced, however, only in schools that follow ITF-style Taekwondo. Kukkiwon-style Taekwondo, for example, does not employ the sine wave and advocates a more uniform height during movements, drawing power mainly from the rotation of the hip.
</p><p>The components of the Theory of Power include:<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">&#91;18&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>Reaction Force: the principle that as the striking limb is brought forward, other parts of the body should be brought backwards in order to provide more power to the striking limb. As an example, if the right leg is brought forward in a <a href="/wiki/Roundhouse_kick" title="Roundhouse kick">roundhouse kick</a>, the right arm is brought backwards to provide the reaction force.</li>
<li>Concentration: the principle of bringing as many muscles as possible to bear on a strike, concentrating the area of impact into as small an area as possible.</li>
<li>Equilibrium: maintaining a correct centre-of-balance throughout a technique.</li>
<li>Breath Control: the idea that during a strike one should exhale, with the exhalation concluding at the moment of impact.</li>
<li>Mass: the principle of bringing as much of the body to bear on a strike as possible; again using the turning kick as an example, the idea would be to rotate the hip as well as the leg during the kick in order to take advantage of the hip's additional mass in terms of providing power to the kick.</li>
<li>Speed: as previously noted, the speed of execution of a technique in Taekwondo is deemed to be even more important than mass in terms of providing power.</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Typical_curriculum">Typical curriculum</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taekwondo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Typical curriculum">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:TAEKWONDO_PRACTICIONER_KORYO.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/TAEKWONDO_PRACTICIONER_KORYO.jpg/220px-TAEKWONDO_PRACTICIONER_KORYO.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="312" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/TAEKWONDO_PRACTICIONER_KORYO.jpg/330px-TAEKWONDO_PRACTICIONER_KORYO.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/14/TAEKWONDO_PRACTICIONER_KORYO.jpg/440px-TAEKWONDO_PRACTICIONER_KORYO.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3209" data-file-height="4548" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:TAEKWONDO_PRACTICIONER_KORYO.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A young blackbelt performs Koryo</div></div></div>
<p>While organizations such as ITF or <a href="/wiki/Kukkiwon" title="Kukkiwon">Kukkiwon</a> define the general style of Taekwondo, individual clubs and schools tend to tailor their Taekwondo practices. Although each Taekwondo club or school is different, a student typically takes part in most or all of the following:
<sup id="cite_ref-Kim_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kim-19">&#91;19&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Poomsae" class="mw-redirect" title="Poomsae">Forms</a> (<i>pumsae</i> / <i>poomsae</i> 품새, <i>hyeong</i> / <i>hyung</i> 형/型 or <i>teul</i> / <i>tul</i> 틀): these serve the same function as <a href="/wiki/Kata" title="Kata">kata</a> in the study of karate</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Sparring" title="Sparring">Sparring</a> (<i>gyeorugi</i> 겨루기 or <i>matseogi</i> 맞서기): sparring includes variations such as freestyle sparring (in which competitors spar without interruption for several minutes); seven-, three-, two-, and one-step sparring (in which students practice pre-arranged sparring combinations); and point sparring (in which sparring is interrupted and then resumed after each point is scored)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Breaking_(martial_arts)" title="Breaking (martial arts)">Breaking</a> (<i>gyeokpa</i> 격파/擊破 or <i>weerok</i>): the breaking of boards is used for testing, training, and martial arts demonstrations. Demonstrations often also incorporate bricks, tiles, and blocks of ice or other materials. These techniques can be separated into three types:
<ul><li>Power breaking – using straightforward techniques to break as many boards as possible</li>
<li>Speed breaking – boards are held loosely by one edge, putting special focus on the speed required to perform the break</li>
<li>Special techniques – breaking fewer boards but by using jumping or <a href="/wiki/Flying_kick" title="Flying kick">flying</a> techniques to attain greater height, distance, or to clear obstacles</li></ul></li>
<li>Self-defense techniques (<i>hosinsul</i> 호신술/護身術)</li>
<li>Learning the fundamental techniques of Taekwondo; these generally include kicks, blocks, punches, and strikes, with somewhat less emphasis on grappling and holds</li>
<li>Throwing and/or falling techniques (<i>deonjigi</i> 던지기 or <i>tteoreojigi</i> 떨어지기)</li>
<li>Both <a href="/wiki/Anaerobic_exercise" title="Anaerobic exercise">anaerobic</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aerobic_exercise" title="Aerobic exercise">aerobic</a> workout, including stretching</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Relaxation_technique" title="Relaxation technique">Relaxation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Meditation" title="Meditation">meditation</a> exercises, as well as <a href="/wiki/Breathing" title="Breathing">breathing</a> control</li>
<li>A focus on mental and ethical <a href="/wiki/Discipline" title="Discipline">discipline</a>, <a href="/wiki/Etiquette" title="Etiquette">etiquette</a>, <a href="/wiki/Justice" title="Justice">justice</a>, <a href="/wiki/Respect" title="Respect">respect</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Self-confidence" title="Self-confidence">self-confidence</a></li>
<li>Examinations to progress to the next rank</li>
<li>Development of personal success and leadership skills</li></ul>
<p>Though weapons training is not a formal part of most Taekwondo federation curricula, individual schools will often incorporate additional training with <a href="/wiki/B%C5%8D" title="Bō">staffs</a>, knives, sticks, etc.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Equipment_and_facilities">Equipment and facilities</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taekwondo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Equipment and facilities">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Dobok-_ITF.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Dobok-_ITF.jpg/250px-Dobok-_ITF.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="157" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Dobok-_ITF.jpg/375px-Dobok-_ITF.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Dobok-_ITF.jpg/500px-Dobok-_ITF.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1280" data-file-height="804" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Dobok-_ITF.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A WT-style <i><a href="/wiki/Dobok" title="Dobok">dobok</a></i></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Dojang_photo.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Dojang_photo.jpg/250px-Dojang_photo.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="166" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Dojang_photo.jpg/375px-Dojang_photo.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Dojang_photo.jpg/500px-Dojang_photo.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3008" data-file-height="2000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Dojang_photo.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A typical <i><a href="/wiki/Dojang" title="Dojang">dojang</a></i></div></div></div>
<p>A Taekwondo practitioner typically wears a uniform (<i>dobok</i> 도복/道服), often white but sometimes black (or other colors), with a belt tied around the waist. White uniforms are considered the traditional color and are usually encouraged for use at formal ceremonies such as belt tests and promotions. Colored uniforms are often reserved for special teams (such as demonstration teams or leadership teams) or higher-level instructors. There are at least three major styles of <i>dobok</i>, with the most obvious differences being in the style of jacket: 
</p>
<ol><li>The cross-over front jacket (usually seen in ITF style), in which the opening of the jacket is vertical.</li>
<li>The cross-over Y-neck jacket (usually seen in the Kukkiwon/WT style, especially for poomase competitions), in which the opening of the jacket crosses the torso diagonally.</li>
<li>The pull-over V-neck jacket (usually seen in Kukkiwon/WT style, especially for sparring competitions).</li></ol>
<p>White uniforms in the Kukkiwon/WT tradition will typically be white throughout the jacket (black trim along the collars only for dan grades), while ITF-style uniforms are usually trimmed with a black border along the collar and bottom of the jacket (for dan grades). The belt color and any insignia thereon indicate the student's rank. Different clubs and schools use different color schemes for belts. In general, the darker the color, the higher the rank. Taekwondo is traditionally performed in bare feet, although martial arts training shoes may sometimes be worn.
</p><p>When sparring, padded equipment is usually worn. In the ITF tradition, typically only the hands and feet are padded. For this reason, ITF sparring often employs only light-contact sparring. In the Kukkiwon/WT tradition, full-contact sparring is facilitated by the employment of more extensive equipment: padded helmets called homyun are always worn, as are padded torso protectors called <a href="/wiki/Hogu" title="Hogu">hogu</a>; feet, shins, groins, hands, and forearms protectors are also worn.
</p><p>The school or place where instruction is given is called the <i><a href="/wiki/Dojang" title="Dojang">dojang</a></i> (도장, 道場). Specifically, the term <i>dojang</i> refers to the area within the school in which martial arts instruction takes place; the word <i>dojang</i> is sometimes translated as <i>gymnasium</i>. In common usage, the term <i>dojang</i> is often used to refer to the school as a whole. Modern <i>dojangs</i> often incorporate padded flooring, often incorporating red-and-blue patterns in the flooring to reflect the colors of the <a href="/wiki/Taegeuk" title="Taegeuk">taegeuk</a> symbol. Some <i>dojangs</i> have wooden flooring instead. The <i>dojang</i> is usually decorated with items such as flags, banners, belts, instructional materials, and traditional Korean calligraphy.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Styles_and_organizations">Styles and organizations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taekwondo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Styles and organizations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:NineKwans_FamilyTree.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/2/2f/NineKwans_FamilyTree.png/250px-NineKwans_FamilyTree.png" decoding="async" width="250" height="204" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/2/2f/NineKwans_FamilyTree.png/375px-NineKwans_FamilyTree.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/2/2f/NineKwans_FamilyTree.png/500px-NineKwans_FamilyTree.png 2x" data-file-width="2808" data-file-height="2291" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:NineKwans_FamilyTree.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A "family tree" illustrating how the five original kwans gave rise to multiple styles of Taekwondo.</div></div></div>
<p>There are a number of major Taekwondo styles as well as a few niche styles. Most styles are associated with a governing body or federation that defines the style. The major technical differences among Taekwondo styles and organizations generally revolve around:
</p>
<ul><li>the <a href="/wiki/Hyeong" title="Hyeong">patterns</a> practiced by each style (called <i>hyeong</i> 형, <i>pumsae</i> 품새, or <i>teul</i> 틀, depending on the style); these are sets of prescribed formal sequences of movements that demonstrate mastery of posture, positioning, and technique</li>
<li>differences in the sparring rules for competition.</li>
<li>martial arts philosophy.</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1946:_Traditional_Taekwondo">1946: Traditional Taekwondo</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taekwondo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: 1946: Traditional Taekwondo">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The term <i>traditional Taekwondo</i> typically refers to martial arts practised in Korea during the 1940s and 1950s by the nine original <a href="/wiki/Kwans" class="mw-redirect" title="Kwans">kwans</a>, or martial arts schools, after the conclusion of the Japanese occupation of Korea at the end of <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>. The term <i>Taekwondo</i> had not yet been coined, and in reality, each of the nine original kwans practised its own style of martial arts. The term <i>traditional Taekwondo</i> serves mostly as an umbrella term for these various styles, as they themselves used various other names such as Tang Soo Do (Chinese Hand Way), Kong Soo Do (Empty Hand Way) and Tae Soo Do (Foot Hand Way). Traditional Taekwondo is still practised today but generally under other names, such as <a href="/wiki/Tang_Soo_Do" title="Tang Soo Do">Tang Soo Do</a> and <a href="/wiki/Soo_Bahk_Do" title="Soo Bahk Do">Soo Bahk Do</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-KANG_6-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-KANG-6">&#91;6&#93;</a></sup> ref name="Gillis" /&gt; In 1959, the name Taekwondo was agreed upon by the nine original kwans as a common term for their martial arts. As part of the unification process, The Korea Taekwondo Association (KTA) was formed through a collaborative effort by representatives from all the kwans, and the work began on a common curriculum, which eventually resulted in the Kukkiwon and the Kukki Style of Taekwondo. The original kwans that formed KTA continues to exist today, but as independent fraternal membership organizations that support the <a href="/wiki/World_Taekwondo" title="World Taekwondo">World Taekwondo</a> and Kukkiwon. The kwans also function as a channel for the issuing of Kukkiwon dan and poom certification (black belt ranks) for their members. The official curriculum of those kwans that joined the unification is that of the Kukkiwon, with the notable exception of half the Oh Do Kwan which joined the ITF instead and therefore uses the Chan Hon curriculum.
</p>
<h3><span id="1966:_ITF.2FChang_Hon-style_Taekwondo"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1966:_ITF/Chang_Hon-style_Taekwondo">1966: ITF/Chang Hon-style Taekwondo</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taekwondo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: 1966: ITF/Chang Hon-style Taekwondo">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/International_Taekwon-Do_Federation" title="International Taekwon-Do Federation">International Taekwon-Do Federation</a> (ITF)-style Taekwondo, more accurately known as Chang Hon-style Taekwondo, is defined by <a href="/wiki/Choi_Hong_Hi" title="Choi Hong Hi">Choi Hong Hi</a>'s <i>Encyclopedia of Taekwon-do</i> published in 1987.<sup id="cite_ref-Choi_17-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Choi-17">&#91;17&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1990, the <a href="/wiki/Global_Taekwondo_Federation" class="mw-redirect" title="Global Taekwondo Federation">Global Taekwondo Federation</a> (GTF) split from the ITF due to the political controversies surrounding the ITF; the GTF continues to practice ITF-style Taekwondo, however, with additional elements incorporated into the style. Likewise, the ITF itself split in 2001 and again in 2002 into three separate federations, headquartered in Austria, the United Kingdom, and Spain respectively.<sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">&#91;20&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">&#91;21&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-22" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-22">&#91;22&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The GTF and all three ITFs practice Choi's ITF-style Taekwondo. In ITF-style Taekwondo, the word used for "forms" is <i><a href="/wiki/Hyeong" title="Hyeong">teul</a></i>; the specific set of teul used by the ITF is called <i>Chang Hon</i>. Choi defined 24 <i>Chang Hon</i> teul. The names and symbolism of the Chang Hon teul refer to elements of <a href="/wiki/History_of_Korea" title="History of Korea">Korean history</a>, culture and religious philosophy. The GTF-variant of ITF practices an additional six teul.
</p><p>Within the ITF Taekwondo tradition there are two sub-styles:
</p>
<ul><li>The style of Taekwondo practised by the ITF before its 1973 split with the KTA is sometimes called by ITF practitioners "traditional Taekwondo", though a more accurate term would be <i>traditional ITF Taekwondo</i>.</li>
<li>After the 1973 split, Choi Hong Hi continued to develop and refine the style, ultimately publishing his work in his 1987 <i>Encyclopedia of Taekwondo</i>. Among the refinements incorporated into this new sub-style is the "sine wave"; one of Choi Hong Hi's later principles of Taekwondo is that the body's centre of gravity should be raised-and-lowered throughout a movement.</li></ul>
<p>Some ITF schools adopt the sine wave style, while others do not. Essentially all ITF schools do, however, use the patterns (teul) defined in the Encyclopedia, with some exceptions related to the forms <i>Juche</i> and <i>Ko-Dang</i>.
</p>
<h3><span id="1969:_ATA.2FSongahm-style_Taekwondo"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1969:_ATA/Songahm-style_Taekwondo">1969: ATA/Songahm-style Taekwondo</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taekwondo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: 1969: ATA/Songahm-style Taekwondo">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1969, <a href="/wiki/Haeng_Ung_Lee" title="Haeng Ung Lee">Haeng Ung Lee</a>, a former Taekwondo instructor in the South Korean military, relocated to Omaha, Nebraska and established a chain of martial arts schools in the United States under the banner of the <a href="/wiki/American_Taekwondo_Association" class="mw-redirect" title="American Taekwondo Association">American Taekwondo Association</a> (ATA). Like Jhoon Rhee Taekwondo, ATA Taekwondo has its roots in traditional Taekwondo. The style of Taekwondo practised by the ATA is called <i>Songahm</i> Taekwondo. The ATA went on to become one of the largest chains of Taekwondo schools in the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">&#91;23&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The ATA established international spin-offs called the <a href="/wiki/Songahm_Taekwondo_Federation" class="mw-redirect" title="Songahm Taekwondo Federation">Songahm Taekwondo Federation</a> (STF) and the <a href="/wiki/World_Traditional_Taekwondo_Union" class="mw-redirect" title="World Traditional Taekwondo Union">World Traditional Taekwondo Union</a> (WTTU) to promote the practice of Songahm Taekwondo internationally.  In 2015, all the spin-offs were reunited under the umbrella of ATA International.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="1970s:_Jhoon_Rhee-style_Taekwondo">1970s: Jhoon Rhee-style Taekwondo</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taekwondo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: 1970s: Jhoon Rhee-style Taekwondo">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In 1962 <a href="/wiki/Jhoon_Rhee" class="mw-redirect" title="Jhoon Rhee">Jhoon Rhee</a> relocated to the United States and established a chain of martial arts schools primarily in the Washington, D.C. area that practised traditional Taekwondo. In the 1970s, at the urging of <a href="/wiki/Choi_Hong_Hi" title="Choi Hong Hi">Choi Hong Hi</a>, Rhee adopted ITF-style Taekwondo within his chain of schools, but like the GTF later departed from the ITF due to the political controversies surrounding Choi and the ITF. Rhee went on to develop his own style of Taekwondo called Jhoon Rhee-style Taekwondo, incorporating elements of both traditional and ITF-style Taekwondo as well as original elements.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">&#91;24&#93;</a></sup> (Note that Jhoon Rhee-style Taekwondo is distinct from the similarly named <a href="/wiki/Rhee_Taekwon-Do" title="Rhee Taekwon-Do">Rhee Taekwon-Do</a>, based in Australia and New Zealand).
</p><p>Jhoon Rhee-style Taekwondo is still practised primarily in the United States and eastern Europe.
</p>
<h3><span id="1972:_Kukki-style_.2F_WT-Taekwondo"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="1972:_Kukki-style_/_WT-Taekwondo">1972: Kukki-style / WT-Taekwondo</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taekwondo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: 1972: Kukki-style / WT-Taekwondo">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Kukkiwon_PoomDan_Comparison.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Kukkiwon_PoomDan_Comparison.png/300px-Kukkiwon_PoomDan_Comparison.png" decoding="async" width="300" height="169" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Kukkiwon_PoomDan_Comparison.png/450px-Kukkiwon_PoomDan_Comparison.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c4/Kukkiwon_PoomDan_Comparison.png/600px-Kukkiwon_PoomDan_Comparison.png 2x" data-file-width="4080" data-file-height="2304" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Kukkiwon_PoomDan_Comparison.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Relative popularity of Kukkiwon-style Taekwondo around the world</div></div></div>
<p>In 1972 the <a href="/wiki/Korea_Taekwondo_Association" title="Korea Taekwondo Association">Korea Taekwondo Association</a> (KTA) Central Dojang opened in Seoul; in 1973 the name was changed to <a href="/wiki/Kukkiwon" title="Kukkiwon">Kukkiwon</a>. Under the sponsorship of the South Korean government's <a href="/wiki/Ministry_of_Culture,_Sports_and_Tourism" title="Ministry of Culture, Sports and Tourism">Ministry of Culture, Sports and Tourism</a> the Kukkiwon became the new national academy for Taekwondo, thereby establishing a new "unified" style of Taekwondo.<sup id="cite_ref-Kukkiwon_14-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kukkiwon-14">&#91;14&#93;</a></sup> In 1973 the KTA established the <a href="/wiki/World_Taekwondo_Federation" class="mw-redirect" title="World Taekwondo Federation">World Taekwondo Federation</a> (WTF, now called <a href="/wiki/World_Taekwondo" title="World Taekwondo">World Taekwondo</a>, WT) to promote the sportive side of Kukki-Taekwondo. The <a href="/wiki/International_Olympic_Committee" title="International Olympic Committee">International Olympic Committee</a> recognized the WT and Taekwondo sparring in 1980. For this reason, the Kukkiwon-defined style of Taekwondo is sometimes referred to as <i>Sport-style</i> Taekwondo, <i>Olympic-style</i> Taekwondo, or <i>WT-style</i> Taekwondo, but the style itself is defined by the Kukkiwon, not by the WT, and the WT competition ruleset itself only allows the use of a very small number of the total number of techniques included in the style.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">&#91;25&#93;</a></sup> Therefore, the correct term for the South Korean government sponsored style of Taekwondo associated with the Kukkiwon, is Kukki Taekwondo, meaning "national Taekwondo" in Korean.
</p><p>The color belts range from white to junior black belt (half black, half red). The order and colours used may vary between schools, but a common order is white, yellow, green, blue, red, black. However, other variations with a higher number of colours is also commonly seen. A usual practice, when employing only four coloured belts, is to stay at each belt color for the duration of two gup ranks, making a total of eight gup ranks between white belt and 1st. dan black belt. In order to make a visual difference between the first and second gup rank of given belt color, a stripe in the same color as the next belt color is added to the second cup rank. 
</p><p>In Kukki-style Taekwondo, the word used for "forms" is <i><a href="/wiki/Hyeong" title="Hyeong">poomsae</a>.</i> In 1967 the KTA established a new set of forms called the <i>Palgwae</i> poomsae, named after the eight trigrams of the <a href="/wiki/I_Ching" title="I Ching">I Ching</a>. In 1971 however (after additional kwans had joined the KTA), the KTA and Kukkiwon adopted a new set of color-belt forms instead, called the <i>Taegeuk</i> poomsae. Black belt forms are called <i>yudanja</i> poomsae. While ITF-style forms refer to key elements of Korean history, Kukki-style forms refer instead to elements of sino-Korean philosophy such as the <a href="/wiki/I_Ching" title="I Ching">I Ching</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Taegeuk" title="Taegeuk">taegeuk</a>.
</p><p>WT-sanctioned tournaments allow any person, regardless of school affiliation or martial arts style, to compete in WT events as long as he or she is a member of the WT Member National Association in his or her nation; this allows essentially anyone to compete in WT-sanctioned competitions.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_styles_and_hybrids">Other styles and hybrids</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taekwondo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Other styles and hybrids">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>As previously mentioned, in 1990 the <a href="/wiki/Global_Taekwondo_Federation" class="mw-redirect" title="Global Taekwondo Federation">Global Taekwondo Federation</a> (GTF) split from the <a href="/wiki/International_Taekwon-Do_Federation" title="International Taekwon-Do Federation">International Taekwon-Do Federation</a> (ITF) to form its own style of Taekwondo based on ITF-style. Essentially this can be considered a variation of ITF-style.
</p><p>Also in 1990, martial artist and actor <a href="/wiki/Chuck_Norris" title="Chuck Norris">Chuck Norris</a>, an alumnus of Hwang Kee's <a href="/wiki/Moo_Duk_Kwan" title="Moo Duk Kwan">Moo Duk Kwan</a> organization, established a hybrid martial art system called <a href="/wiki/Chun_Kuk_Do" class="mw-redirect" title="Chun Kuk Do">Chun Kuk Do</a>. Chun Kuk Do shares many techniques, forms and names with <a href="/wiki/Tang_Soo_Do" title="Tang Soo Do">Tang Soo Do</a> and Taekwondo, and so can be considered a variation of traditional Taekwondo. Similarly, Lim Ching Sing's Hup Kwon Do and Kwang-jo Choi's <a href="/wiki/Choi_Kwang_Do" class="mw-redirect" title="Choi Kwang Do">Choi Kwang Do</a> also derive from Taekwondo.
</p><p>Additionally, there are <i>hybrid</i> martial arts that combine Taekwondo with other styles. These include:
</p>
<ul><li>Extreme Taekwondo: a complex version of World Taekwondo Federation, which combines elements from all Taekwondo styles, Tricking (martial arts), similarities from other martial arts</li>
<li>Kun Gek Do<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">&#91;26&#93;</a></sup> (also Gwon Gyokdo): combines Taekwondo and <a href="/wiki/Muay_thai" class="mw-redirect" title="Muay thai">muay thai</a>.</li>
<li>Han Moo Do: Scandinavian martial art that combines Taekwondo, <a href="/wiki/Hapkido" title="Hapkido">hapkido</a>, and hoi jeon moo sool.</li>
<li>Han Mu Do: Korean martial art that combines Taekwondo and <a href="/wiki/Hapkido" title="Hapkido">hapkido</a>.</li>
<li>Teukgong Moosool: Korean martial art that combines elements of Taekwondo, <a href="/wiki/Hapkido" title="Hapkido">hapkido</a>, <a href="/wiki/Judo" title="Judo">judo</a>, kyuk too ki, and Chinese martial arts.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Yongmudo" class="mw-redirect" title="Yongmudo">Yongmudo</a>: developed at Korea's Yong-In University, combines Taekwondo, <a href="/wiki/Hapkido" title="Hapkido">hapkido</a>, <a href="/wiki/Judo" title="Judo">judo</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Ssireum" title="Ssireum">ssireum</a>.</li></ul>
<h2><span id="Forms_.28patterns.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Forms_(patterns)">Forms (patterns)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taekwondo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Forms (patterns)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Three Korean terms may be used with reference to Taekwondo forms or patterns. These forms are equivalent to <i><a href="/wiki/Kata" title="Kata">kata</a></i> in karate.
</p>
<ul><li><i>Hyeong</i> (sometimes romanized as <i>hyung</i>) is the term usually used in traditional Taekwondo (i.e., 1950s–1960s styles of Korean martial arts).</li>
<li><i>Poomsae</i> (sometimes romanized as <i>pumsae</i> or <i>poomse</i>) is the term officially used by Kukkiwon/WT-style and ATA-style Taekwondo.</li>
<li><i>Teul</i> (sometimes romanized as <i>tul</i>) is the term usually used in ITF/Chang Hon-style Taekwondo.</li></ul>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/Hyeong" title="Hyeong">hyeong</a> is a systematic, prearranged sequence of martial techniques that is performed either with or without the use of a weapon. In <i>dojangs</i> (Taekwondo training gymnasiums) hyeong are used primarily as a form of interval training that is useful in developing <a href="/wiki/Mushin_(mental_state)" title="Mushin (mental state)">mushin</a>, proper kinetics and mental and physical fortitude. Hyeong may resemble combat, but are artistically non-combative and woven together so as to be an effective conditioning tool. One's aptitude for a particular hyeong may be evaluated in competition. In such competitions, hyeong are evaluated by a panel of judges who base the score on many factors including energy, precision, speed, and control. In Western competitions, there are two general classes of hyeong: creative and standard. Creative hyeong are created by the performer and are generally acrobatic in nature and do not necessarily reflect the kinetic principles intrinsic in any martial system.
</p><p>Different Taekwondo styles and associations (ATA, ITF, GTF, WT, etc.) use different Taekwondo forms. Even within a single association, different schools in the association may use slightly different variations on the forms or use different names for the same form (especially in older styles of Taekwondo). This is especially true for beginner forms, which tend to be less standardized than mainstream forms.
</p>
<table class="wikitable collapsible auto-collapse">
