<h2><span id="Ranks.2C_belts.2C_and_promotion"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Ranks,_belts,_and_promotion"><span id="Ranks"></span> Ranks, belts, and promotion</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taekwondo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Ranks, belts, and promotion">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:04232012box_pilates059.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/04232012box_pilates059.JPG/220px-04232012box_pilates059.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/04232012box_pilates059.JPG/330px-04232012box_pilates059.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/04232012box_pilates059.JPG/440px-04232012box_pilates059.JPG 2x" data-file-width="3008" data-file-height="2000" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:04232012box_pilates059.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Exhibition of Taekwondo students at the <a href="/wiki/Monterrey_Institute_of_Technology_and_Higher_Education,_Mexico_City" class="mw-redirect" title="Monterrey Institute of Technology and Higher Education, Mexico City">Monterrey Institute of Technology and Higher Education, Mexico City</a></div></div></div>
<p>Taekwondo ranks vary from style to style and are not standardized. Typically, these ranks are separated into "junior" and "senior" sections, colloquially referred to as "color belts" and "black belts":
</p>
<ul><li>The junior section of ranks—the "color belt" ranks—are indicated by the Korean word <i><a href="/wiki/Geup" class="mw-redirect" title="Geup">geup</a></i> <span lang="ko" title="Korean language text">급  (級)</span> (also Romanized as <i>gup</i> or <i>kup</i>). Practitioners in these ranks generally wear belts ranging in color from white (the lowest rank) to red or brown  (higher ranks, depending on the style of Taekwondo). Belt colors may be solid or may include a colored stripe on a solid background. The number of geup ranks varies depending on the style, typically ranging between 8 and 12 geup ranks. The numbering sequence for geup ranks usually begins at the larger number of white belts, and then counts down to "1st geup" as the highest color-belt rank.</li>
<li>The senior section of ranks—the "<a href="/wiki/Black_belt_(martial_arts)" title="Black belt (martial arts)">black belt</a>" ranks—is typically made up of nine ranks. Each rank is called a <i><a href="/wiki/Dan_(rank)" title="Dan (rank)">dan</a></i> <span lang="ko" title="Korean language text">단 (段)</span> or "degree" (as in "third <i>dan</i>" or "third-degree black belt"). The numbering sequence for dan ranks is opposite that of geup ranks: numbering begins at 1st dan (the lowest black-belt rank) and counts upward for higher ranks. A practitioner's degree is sometimes indicated on the belt itself with stripes, Roman numerals, or other methods.</li></ul>
<p>Some styles incorporate an additional rank between the geup and dan levels, called the "bo-dan" rank—essentially, a candidate rank for black belt promotion. Additionally, the Kukkiwon/WT-style of Taekwondo recognizes a "poom" rank for practitioners under the age of 15: these practitioners have passed dan-level tests but will not receive dan-level rank until age 15. At age 15, their poom rank is considered to transition to equivalent dan rank automatically. In some schools, holders of the poom rank wear a half-red/half-black belt rather than a solid black belt.
</p><p>To advance from one rank to the next, students typically complete <i>promotion tests</i> in which they demonstrate their proficiency in the various aspects of the art before their teacher or a panel of judges. Promotion tests vary from school to school, but may include such elements as the execution of patterns, which combine various techniques in specific sequences; the breaking of boards to demonstrate the ability to use techniques with both power and control; sparring and self-defense to demonstrate the practical application and control of techniques; physical fitness usually with push-ups and sit-ups; and answering questions on terminology, concepts, and history to demonstrate knowledge and understanding of the art. For higher <i>dan</i> tests, students are sometimes required to take a written test or submit a research paper in addition to taking the practical test.
</p><p>Promotion from one <i>geup</i> to the next can proceed rapidly in some <a href="/wiki/School" title="School">schools</a> since schools often allow <i>geup</i> promotions every two, three, or four months. Students of <i>geup</i> rank learn the most basic techniques first, and then move on to more advanced techniques as they approach first <i>dan</i>. Many of the older and more traditional schools often take longer to allow students to test for higher ranks than newer, more contemporary schools, as they may not have the required testing intervals. In contrast, promotion from one <i>dan</i> to the next can take years. In fact, some styles impose age or time-in-rank limits on dan promotions. For example, the number of years between one dan promotion to the next may be limited to a minimum of the practitioner's current dan-rank, so that (for example) a 5th dan practitioner must wait 5 years to test for 6th dan.
</p><p>Black belt ranks may have titles associated with them, such as "master" and "instructor", but Taekwondo organizations vary widely in rules and standards when it comes to ranks and titles. What holds true in one organization may not hold true in another, as is the case in many <a href="/wiki/Martial_art" class="mw-redirect" title="Martial art">martial art</a> systems. For example, achieving first <i>dan ( black belt)</i> ranking with three years' training might be typical in one organization but considered too quick in another organization, and likewise for other ranks. Similarly, the title for a given <i>dan</i> rank in one organization might not be the same as the title for that <i>dan</i> rank in another organization.
</p><p>In the <a href="/wiki/International_Taekwon-Do_Federation" title="International Taekwon-Do Federation">International Taekwon-Do Federation</a>, instructors holding 1st to 3rd <i>dan</i> are called <i>Boosabum</i> (assistant instructor), those holding 4th to 6th <i>dan</i> are called <i>Sabum</i> (instructor), those holding 7th to 8th <i>dan</i> are called <i>Sahyun</i> (master), and those holding 9th <i>dan</i> are called <i>Saseong</i> (grandmaster).<sup id="cite_ref-Choi1993-1.122_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Choi1993-1.122-33">&#91;33&#93;</a></sup> This system does not, however, necessarily apply to other Taekwondo organizations.
</p><p>In the American Taekwondo Association, instructor designations are separate from rank.  Black belts may be designated as an instructor trainee (red, white and blue collar), specialty trainer (red and black collar), certified trainer (black-red-black collar) and certified instructor (black collar).  After a one-year waiting period, instructors who hold the sixth dan are eligible for the title of Master.  Seventh dan black belts are eligible for the title Senior Master and eighth dan black belts are eligible for the title Chief Master.
</p><p>In WT/Kukki-Taekwondo, instructors holding 1st. to 3rd. <i>dan</i> are considered assistant instructors (kyosa-nim), are not yet allowed to issue ranks, and are generally thought of as still having much to learn. Instructors who hold a 4th. to 6th. <i>dan</i> are considered master instructors (sabum-nim), and is allowed to grade students to color belt ranks from 4th. dan, and to black belt/dan-ranks from 6th. dan. Those who hold a 7th–9th <i>dan</i> are considered Grandmasters. These ranks also holds an age requirement of 40+.<sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">&#91;34&#93;</a></sup> In this style, a 10th dan rank is sometimes awarded posthumously for practitioners with a lifetime of demonstrable contributions to the practice of Taekwondo.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Historical_influences">Historical influences</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taekwondo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Historical influences">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Korean_martial_arts" title="Korean martial arts">Korean martial arts</a></div>
<p>The oldest <a href="/wiki/Korea" title="Korea">Korean</a> martial arts were an amalgamation of unarmed combat styles developed by the three rival Korean Kingdoms of <a href="/wiki/Goguryeo" title="Goguryeo">Goguryeo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Silla" title="Silla">Silla</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Baekje" title="Baekje">Baekje</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-SPIRIT_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SPIRIT-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup> where young men were trained in unarmed combat techniques to develop strength, speed, and survival skills. The most popular of these techniques were <a href="/wiki/Ssireum" title="Ssireum">ssireum</a>, <a href="/wiki/Subak" title="Subak">subak</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Taekkyeon" title="Taekkyeon">Taekkyon</a>. The Northern Goguryeo kingdom was a dominant force in Northern Korea and North Eastern China prior to the 1st century CE, and again from the 3rd century to the 6th century. Before the fall of the Goguryeo Dynasty in the 6th century, the Shilla Kingdom asked for help in training its people for defence against pirate invasions. During this time a few select Silla warriors were given training in <a href="/wiki/Taekkyeon" title="Taekkyeon">Taekkyon</a> by the early masters from <a href="/wiki/Goguryeo" title="Goguryeo">Goguryeo</a>. These Silla warriors then became known as <a href="/wiki/Hwarang" title="Hwarang">Hwarang</a> or "blossoming knights." The Hwarang set up a military academy for the sons of royalty in Silla called <a href="/wiki/Hwarang" title="Hwarang">Hwarang-do</a> {花郎徒}, which means "flower-youth corps." The Hwarang studied Taekkyon, history, <a href="/wiki/Confucian" class="mw-redirect" title="Confucian">Confucian</a> philosophy, ethics, <a href="/wiki/Buddhist" class="mw-redirect" title="Buddhist">Buddhist</a> morality, social skills, and military tactics. The guiding principles of the <a href="/wiki/Hwarang" title="Hwarang">Hwarang</a> warriors were based on <a href="/wiki/Won_Gwang" class="mw-redirect" title="Won Gwang">Won Gwang</a>'s five codes of human conduct and included loyalty, filial duty, trustworthiness, valour, and justice.
<sup id="cite_ref-Seth_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Seth-36">&#91;36&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In spite of Korea's rich history of ancient and martial arts, Korean martial arts faded during the late <a href="/wiki/Joseon_Dynasty" class="mw-redirect" title="Joseon Dynasty">Joseon Dynasty</a>. Korean society became highly centralized under <a href="/wiki/Korean_Confucianism" title="Korean Confucianism">Korean Confucianism</a>, and martial arts were poorly regarded in a society whose ideals were epitomized by its scholar-kings.<sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">&#91;37&#93;</a></sup> Formal practices of traditional martial arts such as <a href="/wiki/Subak" title="Subak">subak</a> and <a href="/wiki/Taekkyeon" title="Taekkyeon">Taekkyon</a> were reserved for sanctioned military uses. However, <a href="/wiki/Taekkyeon" title="Taekkyeon">Taekkyon</a> persisted into the 19th century as a folk game during the May-Dano festival, and was still taught as the formal military martial art throughout the Joseon Dynasty.<sup id="cite_ref-SPIRIT_35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-SPIRIT-35">&#91;35&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Early progenitors of Taekwondo—the founders of the nine original <a href="/wiki/Kwans" class="mw-redirect" title="Kwans">kwans</a>—who were able to study in Japan were exposed to <a href="/wiki/Japanese_martial_arts" title="Japanese martial arts">Japanese martial arts</a>, including <a href="/wiki/Karate" title="Karate">karate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Judo" title="Judo">judo</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Kendo" title="Kendo">kendo</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-Park_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Park-38">&#91;38&#93;</a></sup> while others were exposed to the martial arts of China and Manchuria, as well as to the indigenous Korean martial art of <a href="/wiki/Taekkyeon" title="Taekkyeon">Taekkyon</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Brief_History_of_Taekwondo_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Brief_History_of_Taekwondo-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-Glen_39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Glen-39">&#91;39&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">&#91;40&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">&#91;41&#93;</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hwang_Kee" title="Hwang Kee">Hwang Kee</a> founder of <a href="/wiki/Moo_Duk_Kwan" title="Moo Duk Kwan">Moo Duk Kwan</a>, further incorporated elements of Korean <a href="/wiki/Gwonbeop" title="Gwonbeop">Gwonbeop</a> from the <a href="/wiki/Muye_Dobo_Tongji" class="mw-redirect" title="Muye Dobo Tongji">Muye Dobo Tongji</a> into the style that eventually became <a href="/wiki/Tang_Soo_Do" title="Tang Soo Do">Tang Soo Do</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Philosophy">Philosophy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taekwondo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Philosophy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Different styles of Taekwondo adopt different philosophical underpinnings. Many of these underpinnings however refer back to the Five Commandments of the <a href="/wiki/Hwarang" title="Hwarang">Hwarang</a> as a historical referent. For example, Choi Hong Hi expressed his philosophical basis for Taekwondo as the <a href="/wiki/International_Taekwon-Do_Federation#Philosophy" title="International Taekwon-Do Federation">Five Tenets of Taekwondo</a>:<sup id="cite_ref-The_Tenants_Of_Tae_Kwon_Do_42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-The_Tenants_Of_Tae_Kwon_Do-42">&#91;42&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>Courtesy (<i>yeui</i> / 예의, 禮儀)</li>
<li>Integrity (<i>yeomchi</i> / 염치, 廉恥)</li>
<li>Perseverance (<i>innae</i> / 인내, 忍耐)</li>
<li>Self-control (<i>geukgi</i> / 극기, 克己)</li>
<li>Indomitable spirit (<i>baekjeolbulgul</i> / 백절불굴, 百折不屈)</li></ul>
<p>These tenets are further articulated in a Taekwondo oath, also authored by Choi:
</p>
<ul><li>I shall observe the tenets of Taekwondo</li>
<li>I shall respect the instructor and seniors</li>
<li>I shall never misuse Taekwondo</li>
<li>I shall be a champion of freedom and justice</li>
<li>I shall build a more peaceful world</li></ul>
<p>Modern ITF organizations have continued to update and expand upon this philosophy.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">&#91;43&#93;</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">&#91;44&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The World Taekwondo Federation (WTF) also refers to the commandments of the Hwarang in the articulation of its Taekwondo philosophy.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">&#91;45&#93;</a></sup> Like the ITF philosophy, it centers on the development of a peaceful society as one of the overarching goals for the practice of Taekwondo. The WT's stated philosophy is that this goal can be furthered by adoption of the Hwarang spirit, by behaving rationally ("education in accordance with the reason of heaven"), and by recognition of the philosophies embodied in the <a href="/wiki/Taegeuk" title="Taegeuk">taegeuk</a> (the yin and the yang, i.e., "the unity of opposites") and the <a href="/wiki/Taegeuk#Tricolored_Taegeuk" title="Taegeuk">sam taegeuk</a> (understanding change in the world as the interactions of the heavens, the Earth, and Man). The philosophical position articulated by the Kukkiwon is likewise based on the Hwarang tradition.<sup id="cite_ref-46" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-46">&#91;46&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Competition">Competition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taekwondo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Competition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Class_sparring.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Class_sparring.jpg/220px-Class_sparring.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Class_sparring.jpg/330px-Class_sparring.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Class_sparring.jpg/440px-Class_sparring.jpg 2x" data-file-width="960" data-file-height="640" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Class_sparring.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Sparring in a Taekwondo class</div></div></div>
<p>Taekwondo competition typically involves <a href="/wiki/Sparring" title="Sparring">sparring</a>, <a href="/wiki/Breaking_(martial_arts)" title="Breaking (martial arts)">breaking</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hyeong" title="Hyeong">patterns</a>; some tournaments also include special events such as demonstration teams and self-defense (<i>hosinsul</i>). In Olympic Taekwondo competition, however, only sparring (using WT competition rules) is performed.<sup id="cite_ref-WTF-rules_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WTF-rules-47">&#91;47&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>There are two kinds of competition sparring: point sparring, in which all strikes are light contact and the clock is stopped when a point is scored; and Olympic sparring, where all strikes are full contact and the clock continues when points are scored. Sparring involves a Hogu, or a chest protector, which muffles any kick's damage to avoid serious injuries. Helmets and other gear are provided as well. Though other systems may vary, a common point system works like this: One point for a regular kick to the Hogu, two for a turning behind the kick, three for a back kick, and four for a spinning kick to the head.
</p>
<h3><span id="World_Taekwondo_.28WT.29_Competition"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="World_Taekwondo_(WT)_Competition">World Taekwondo (WT) Competition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taekwondo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: World Taekwondo (WT) Competition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Proteccionestkd.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Proteccionestkd.JPG/220px-Proteccionestkd.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="293" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Proteccionestkd.JPG/330px-Proteccionestkd.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Proteccionestkd.JPG/440px-Proteccionestkd.JPG 2x" data-file-width="480" data-file-height="640" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Proteccionestkd.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Official WT trunk protector (<i><a href="/wiki/Hogu" title="Hogu">hogu</a></i>), forearm guards and shin guards</div></div></div>
<p>Under World Taekwondo (WT, formerly WTF) and Olympic rules, sparring is a full-contact event, employing a continuous scoring system where the fighters are allowed to continue after scoring each technique, taking place between two competitors in either an area measuring 8&#160; meters square or an octagon of similar size.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> Competitors are matched within gender and weight division—eight divisions for World Championships that are condensed to four for the Olympics. A win can occur by points, or if one competitor is unable to continue (knockout). However, there are several decisions that can lead to a win, as well, including superiority, withdrawal, disqualification, or even a referee's punitive declaration.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">&#91;49&#93;</a></sup>  Each match consists of three two-minute rounds, with one minute rest between rounds, though these are often abbreviated or shortened for some junior and regional tournaments.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_48-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup> Competitors must wear a <a href="/wiki/Hogu" title="Hogu">hogu</a>, head protector, shin pads, foot socks, forearm guards, hand gloves, a mouthpiece, and a groin cup. Tournaments sanctioned by national governing bodies or the WT, including the Olympics and World Championship, use electronic hogus, electronic foot socks, and electronic head protectors to register and determine scoring techniques, with human judges used to assess and score technical (spinning) techniques and score punches.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_48-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Points are awarded for permitted techniques delivered to the legal scoring areas as determined by an electronic scoring system, which assesses the strength and location of the contact. The only techniques allowed are kicks (delivering a strike using an area of the foot below the ankle), punches (delivering a strike using the closed fist), and pushes. In some smaller tournaments, and in the past, points were awarded by three corner judges using electronic scoring tallies. All major national and international tournaments have moved fully (as of 2017) to electronic scoring, including the use of electronic headgear. This limits corner judges to scoring only technical points and punches. Some believe that the new electronic scoring system reduces controversy concerning judging decisions,<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">&#91;50&#93;</a></sup> but this technology is still not universally accepted.,<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">&#91;51&#93;</a></sup> In particular, the move to electronic headgear has replaced controversy over judging with controversy over how the technology has changed the sport. Because the headgear is not able to determine if a kick was a correct Taekwondo technique, and the pressure threshold for sensor activation for headgear is kept low for safety reasons, athletes who improvised ways of placing their foot on their opponents head were able to score points, regardless of how true to Taekwondo those techniques were.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">&#91;52&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Techniques are divided into three categories: scoring techniques (such as a kick to the hogu), permitted but non-scoring techniques (such as a kick that strikes an arm), and not-permitted techniques (such as a kick below the waist).
</p>
<ul><li>A punch that makes strong contact with the opponent's <i><a href="/wiki/Hogu" title="Hogu">hogu</a></i> scores 1 point. The punch must be a straight punch with arm extended; jabs, hooks, uppercuts, etc. are permitted but do not score. Punches to the head are not allowed.</li>
<li>A regular kick (no turning or spinning) to the hogu scores 2 points.</li>
<li>A regular kick (no turning or spinning) to the head scores 3 points</li>
<li>A technical kick (a kick that involves turning or spinning) to the hogu scores 4 points.</li>
<li>A technical kick to the head scores 5 points.
<ul><li>As of October 2010, 4 points were awarded if a turning kick was used to execute this attack. As of June 2018, this was changed to 5 points.<sup id="cite_ref-WTFCompetitionRulesInterpretation2010b_53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WTFCompetitionRulesInterpretation2010b-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup></li></ul></li></ul>
<p>The referee can give penalties at any time for rule-breaking, such as hitting an area not recognized as a target, usually the legs or neck. Penalties, called "Gam-jeom" are counted as an addition of one point for the opposing contestant. Following 10 "Gam-jeom" a player is declared the loser by referee's punitive declaration<sup id="cite_ref-:0_48-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>At the end of three rounds, the competitor with most points wins the match. In the event of a tie, a fourth "sudden death" overtime round, sometimes called a "Golden Point", is held to determine the winner after a one-minute rest period. In this round, the first competitor to score a point wins the match. If there is no score in the additional round, the winner is decided by superiority, as determined by the refereeing officials<sup id="cite_ref-WTFCompetitionRulesInterpretation2010b_53-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WTFCompetitionRulesInterpretation2010b-53">&#91;53&#93;</a></sup> or number of fouls committed during that round.
</p><p>If a competitor has a 20-point lead at the end of the second round or achieves a 20-point lead at any point in the third round, then the match is over and that competitor is declared the winner.<sup id="cite_ref-:0_48-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:0-48">&#91;48&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>In addition to sparring competition, World Taekwondo sanctions competition in poomsae or forms, although this is not an Olympic event. Single competitors perform a designated pattern of movements, and are assessed by judges for accuracy (accuracy of movements, balance, precision of details) and presentation (speed and power, rhythm, energy), both of which receive numerical scores, with deductions made for errors.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup> Pair and team competition is also recognized, where two or more competitors perform the same form at the same time. In addition to competition with the traditional forms, there is experimentation with freestyle forms that allow more creativity.<sup id="cite_ref-:1_54-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-:1-54">&#91;54&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>The World Taekwondo Federation directly sanctions the following competitions:<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">&#91;55&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>World Taekwondo Poomsae Championships</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/World_Taekwondo_Championships" title="World Taekwondo Championships">World Taekwondo Championships</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/World_Para_Taekwondo_Championships" class="mw-redirect" title="World Para Taekwondo Championships">World Para Taekwondo Championships</a> (since 2009)<sup id="cite_ref-56" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-56">&#91;56&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>World Taekwondo Cadet Championships</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/World_Taekwondo_Junior_Championships" title="World Taekwondo Junior Championships">World Taekwondo Junior Championships</a></li>
<li>World Taekwondo Team Championships</li>
<li>World Taekwondo Para Championships</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/World_Taekwondo_Grand_Prix" title="World Taekwondo Grand Prix">World Taekwondo Grand Prix</a></li>
<li>World Taekwondo Beach Championships</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Taekwondo_at_the_Summer_Olympics" title="Taekwondo at the Summer Olympics">Olympic Games</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Paralympic_Games" title="Paralympic Games">Paralympic Games</a> (debut in 2020 Tokyo Paralympics)<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">&#91;57&#93;</a></sup></li></ul>
<h3><span id="International_Taekwon-Do_Federation_.28ITF.29_Competition"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="International_Taekwon-Do_Federation_(ITF)_Competition">International Taekwon-Do Federation (ITF) Competition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taekwondo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: International Taekwon-Do Federation (ITF) Competition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:ITF_TaeKwon-Do_Sparring_Gear.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/2/28/ITF_TaeKwon-Do_Sparring_Gear.JPG/220px-ITF_TaeKwon-Do_Sparring_Gear.JPG" decoding="async" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/2/28/ITF_TaeKwon-Do_Sparring_Gear.JPG/330px-ITF_TaeKwon-Do_Sparring_Gear.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/2/28/ITF_TaeKwon-Do_Sparring_Gear.JPG/440px-ITF_TaeKwon-Do_Sparring_Gear.JPG 2x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1200" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:ITF_TaeKwon-Do_Sparring_Gear.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Common styles of ITF point sparring equipment</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/International_Taekwon-Do_Federation" title="International Taekwon-Do Federation">International Taekwon-Do Federation</a>'s sparring rules are similar to the WT's rules but differ in several aspects.
</p>
<ul><li>Hand attacks to the head are allowed.<sup id="cite_ref-itf_information10c_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-itf_information10c-58">&#91;58&#93;</a></sup></li>
<li>The competition is not full contact, and excessive contact is not allowed.</li>
<li>Competitors are penalized with disqualification if they injure their opponent and he can no longer continue (knockout).</li>
<li>The scoring system is:
<ul><li>1 point for Punch to the body or head.</li>
<li>2 points for Jumping kick to the body or kick to the head, or a jumping punch to the head</li>
<li>3 points for Jumping kick to the head</li></ul></li>
<li>The competition area is 9×9 meters for international events.</li></ul>
<p>Competitors do not wear the <i>hogu</i> (although they are required to wear approved foot and hand protection equipment, as well as optional head guards). This scoring system varies between individual organisations within the ITF; for example, in the TAGB, punches to the head or body score 1 point, kicks to the body score 2 points, and kicks to the head score 3 points.
</p><p>A continuous point system is utilized in ITF competition, where the fighters are allowed to continue after scoring a technique. Excessive contact is generally not allowed according to the official ruleset, and judges penalize any competitor with disqualification if they injure their opponent and he can no longer continue (although these rules vary between ITF organizations). At the end of two minutes (or some other specified time), the competitor with more scoring techniques wins.
</p><p>Fouls in ITF sparring include: attacking a fallen opponent, leg sweeping, holding/grabbing, or intentional attack to a target other than the opponent.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">&#91;59&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>ITF competitions also feature performances of patterns, <a href="/wiki/Breaking_(martial_arts)" title="Breaking (martial arts)">breaking</a>, and 'special techniques' (where competitors perform prescribed board breaks at great heights).
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Multi-discipline_competition">Multi-discipline competition</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taekwondo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Multi-discipline competition">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Some organizations deliver multi-discipline competitions, for example the <a href="/wiki/British_Student_Taekwondo_Federation" title="British Student Taekwondo Federation">British Student Taekwondo Federation</a>'s inter-university competitions, which have included separate WT rules sparring, ITF rules sparring, Kukkiwon patterns and Chang-Hon patterns events run in parallel since 1992.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">&#91;60&#93;</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_organizations">Other organizations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taekwondo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Other organizations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/United_States" title="United States">American</a> <a href="/wiki/Amateur_Athletic_Union" title="Amateur Athletic Union">Amateur Athletic Union</a> (AAU) competitions are very similar, except that different styles of pads and gear are allowed.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">&#91;61&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>Apart from WT and ITF tournaments, major Taekwondo competitions (all featuring WT Taekwondo only) include:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Universiade" title="Universiade">Universiade</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Asian_Games" title="Asian Games">Asian Games</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/African_Games" title="African Games">African Games</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/European_Games" title="European Games">European Games</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Pan_American_Games" title="Pan American Games">Pan American Games</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Pacific_Games" title="Pacific Games">Pacific Games</a></li></ul>
<p>Taekwondo is also an optional sport at the <a href="/wiki/Commonwealth_Games" title="Commonwealth Games">Commonwealth Games</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Korean_Taekwondo_vocabulary">Korean Taekwondo vocabulary</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Taekwondo&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Korean Taekwondo vocabulary">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Taekwondo_Vocabulary.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/e7/Taekwondo_Vocabulary.png/250px-Taekwondo_Vocabulary.png" decoding="async" width="250" height="188" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/e7/Taekwondo_Vocabulary.png/375px-Taekwondo_Vocabulary.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/e7/Taekwondo_Vocabulary.png/500px-Taekwondo_Vocabulary.png 2x" data-file-width="6116" data-file-height="4591" /></a>  <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Taekwondo_Vocabulary.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Some common Taekwondo terminology and parts of the body</div></div></div>
<p>In Taekwondo schools—even outside Korea—<a href="/wiki/Korean_language" title="Korean language">Korean language</a> commands and vocabulary are often used. <a href="/wiki/Korean_numerals" title="Korean numerals">Korean numerals</a> may be used as prompts for commands or for counting repetition exercises. Different schools and associations will use different vocabulary, however, and may even refer to entirely different techniques by the same name. As one example, in Kukkiwon/WT-style Taekwondo, the term <i>ap seogi</i> refers to an upright walking stance, while in ITF/Chang Hon-style Taekwondo <i>ap seogi</i> refers to a long, low, front stance. Korean vocabulary commonly used in Taekwondo schools includes:
</p>
<table class="wikitable mw-collapsible mw-collapsed">

<tbody><tr>
<th colspan="4">Basic Commands
</th></tr>
<tr>
<th>English</th>
<th>Hangul (한글)</th>
<th>Hanja (한자/漢字)</th>
<th><a href="/wiki/Revised_Romanization" class="mw-redirect" title="Revised Romanization">Revised Romanization</a>
</th></tr>

<td>격파</td>
<td>擊破</td>
<td>Gyeog pa
</td></tr></tbody></table>

<li id="cite_note-:3-10"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-:3_10-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://gtaitf-india.com/general-choi-hong-hi/">"General Choi, utilizing both his advanced education and Calligraphy skills that involved extensive knowledge of Chinese characters and language, searched for and later conceived of the new term Tae Kwon Do. <li id="cite_note-11"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-11">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://akillingart.com/namtaehialexgillistaekwondo-namedi-n-1955-2min/">"Interview with Nam Tae-Hi making it clear that Tae Kwon Do came from Korean Karate (also known as "Shotokan Karate," "Tang Soo Do" and "Kong Soo Do"). <li id="cite_note-12"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-12">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web">Lo, David. <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.members.itkd.co.nz/reference/essays/6-broken_family.pdf">"Nam and General Choi faced a dilemma as they could not teach the Koreans Karate and call it Taekkyon. (2000). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.martialartsresource.com/anonftp/pub/the_dojang/digests/spirit.html"><i>Taekwondo: The Spirit of Korea (portions of)</i></a>. Korea: Ministry of Culture and Tourism, Republic of Korea. <q>Korea has a long history of martial arts stretching well back into ancient times. Written historical records from the early days of the Korean peninsula are sparse, however, there are a number of well-preserved archaeological artefacts that tell stores of Korea's early martial arts.", "taekwondo leaders started to experiment with a radical new system that would result in the development of a new martial sport different from anything ever seen before. This new martial sport would bear some important similarities to the traditional Korean game of taekkyon.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.btitle=Taekwondo%3A+The+Spirit+of+Korea+%28portions+of%29&amp;rft.place=Korea&amp;rft.pub=Ministry+of+Culture+and+Tourism%2C+Republic+of+Korea&amp;rft.date=2000&amp;rft.aulast=Capener&amp;rft.aufirst=Steven+D.&amp;rft.au=H.+Edward+Kim+%28ed.%29&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.martialartsresource.com%2Fanonftp%2Fpub%2Fthe_dojang%2Fdigests%2Fspirit.html&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ATaekwondo" class="Z3988"></span><span class="cs1-maint citation-comment">CS1 maint: extra text: authors list (<a href="/wiki/Category:CS1_maint:_extra_text:_authors_list" title="Category:CS1 maint: extra text: authors list">link</a>)</span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/></span>

<li id="cite_note-41"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-41">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web"><a href="/wiki/Choi_Hong_Hi" title="Choi Hong Hi">Choi Hong Hi</a> (1999). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20090918100957/http://www.itf-information.com/information02.htm">"ITF Information interviews with General Choi"</a>. Archived from <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.itf-information.com/information02.htm">the original</a> on 2009-09-18. <q>Young Choi's father sent him to study calligraphy under one of the most famous teachers in Korea, Mr Han II Dong. Han, in addition to his skills as a calligrapher, was also a master of taekkyeon, the ancient Korean art of foot fighting. The teacher, concerned over the frail condition of his new student, began teaching him the rigorous exercises of taekkyeon to help build up his body.</q></cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.btitle=ITF+Information+interviews+with+General+Choi.&amp;rft.pub=The+Condensed+Encyclopedia+Fifth+Edition&amp;rft.date=1999&amp;rft.au=Choi+Hong+Hi&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.itf-information.com%2Finformation02.htm&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3ATaekwondo" class="Z3988"></span><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r886058088"/></span>
